  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_66a271db-314d-414e-b793-421219786024'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer10235053773545525076">While an advanced physics graduate student at one of the most prestigious universities in Europe, Elisa Robledo, a young physics professor was invited to join a select research team working on manipulating String Theory, making it possible to witness images of the past as if they were live and actually happening. According to the team's research, breaking down particles of</span>
  <span id="freeText10235053773545525076" style="display:none">While an advanced physics graduate student at one of the most prestigious universities in Europe, Elisa Robledo, a young physics professor was invited to join a select research team working on manipulating String Theory, making it possible to witness images of the past as if they were live and actually happening. According to the team's research, breaking down particles of light, and accessing the code hidden within each fragment, they theorized that the possibility of witnessing the past, viewing such milestone events as of the crucifixion of Christ, or the earth when dinosaurs still roamed. Scurried away on a remote island in the Indian Ocean, they made leaping advancements in their analysis. Yet, their experiments resulted in something much more frightening and dangerous than any of them could have ever imagined. The team awoke something dangerous in their meddling into the fluctuation of Time.<br /><br />Now, years later, Elisa is faced with solving the mysterious and gruesome deaths of each member of the team she was once so proud to be a part of. Something or someone has focused their sights on Elisa and her former academic fellowship. In order to solve the mystery behind what the team's experiments awoke and just who or what is behind the dark forces trailing the once team's every move, Elisa must discover what really happened on the island where her team was once sequestered, and where she had naively thought their dedicated science was meant for good.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10235053773545525076" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <a title="Tiffany PSquared" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27443399-tiffany-psquared"><img alt="Tiffany PSquared" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1492731992p2/27443399.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1985264168?book_show_action=true">Apr 28, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tiffany PSquared" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tiffany PSquared" href="/user/show/27443399-tiffany-psquared">Tiffany PSquared</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780061193736" href="/book/show/4342761-zig-zag">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27443399-tiffany-psquared?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27443399-tiffany-psquared?shelf=sci-fi-or-fantasy">sci-fi-or-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27443399-tiffany-psquared?shelf=2018-reading-challenge">2018-reading-challenge</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1985264168" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17091362468372900818"><b>"Scientists are not after the truth; it is the truth that is after the scientists. - Karl Schlecta"</b><br/><br/>Advanced Physics student Elisa Robledo was selected to research Project Zig Zag with a team of some of the greatest scientific minds on the planet. But 10 years after the project was unexpectedly terminated, her colleagues are suffering horrifying deaths one-by-one, and she gets a terrifying phone call that confirms that she could be next.<br/><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4342761.Zig_Zag' title='Zig Zag by Jos√© Carlos Somoza' rel='nofollow noopener'>Zig Zag</a> is sci-fi deeply entrenched in physics and the possi</span>
  <span id="freeText17091362468372900818" style="display:none"><b>"Scientists are not after the truth; it is the truth that is after the scientists. - Karl Schlecta"</b><br /><br />Advanced Physics student Elisa Robledo was selected to research Project Zig Zag with a team of some of the greatest scientific minds on the planet. But 10 years after the project was unexpectedly terminated, her colleagues are suffering horrifying deaths one-by-one, and she gets a terrifying phone call that confirms that she could be next.<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4342761.Zig_Zag" title="Zig Zag by Jos√© Carlos Somoza" rel="nofollow noopener">Zig Zag</a> is sci-fi deeply entrenched in physics and the possibilities of viewing events of the past - for research purposes, of course...<br /><br /><b>Hits:</b> The science is lofty, but not written in a way that makes non-scientist's brains blank out and scramble. Each character appears to be equally deception. There are enough secrets to go around, and we aren't completely familiar enough with each character to know who's innocent and who's a maniacal supernatural serial killer. The diversity of the characters - Spanish, American, Russian, etc. I love it when a cast goes international. üòÄ<br /><br /><b>Misses:</b> If I had to read about how beautiful and sexy and shapely the MC was, I was gonna gag (I actually think I did a few times). Trust me, we get it. She's pretty, all the men wanted to sleep with her. MOVE ON! And we definitely got robbed on the big reveal of the outcome of the project. Only 3 past events? Come on! There was SO much we could have seen! Finally, the ending. Um... less than satisfying.<br /><br />Three stars, because it held my attention and I kept anticipating a huge dramatic ending that was only partially delivered (points for a slightly unexpected twist).</span>
  <a data-text-id="17091362468372900818" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2434252311" />
    <a title="Chris_P" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44309339-chris-p"><img alt="Chris_P" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1558554241p2/44309339.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2434252311?book_show_action=true">Jun 29, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris_P" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris_P" href="/user/show/44309339-chris-p">Chris_P</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789601627656" href="/book/show/13507059">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44309339-chris-p?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2434252311" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6702101402713340538"><b>Juan Carlos Somoza ‚Äì Zig Zag</b><br/>Another brilliant idea not so brilliantly executed. It‚Äôs rare to come across such original ideas nowadays, especially in the genre of science fiction, which makes the disappointment twice as big to see it wasted in clich√©s and cheap tricks. I guess what bothered me most was the fact that Somoza objectified his own heroin. And although the objectification makes sense -quite cleverly, one might say- in the end, the uneasiness remains, highlighted by the lack of a redemp</span>
  <span id="freeText6702101402713340538" style="display:none"><b>Juan Carlos Somoza ‚Äì Zig Zag</b><br />Another brilliant idea not so brilliantly executed. It‚Äôs rare to come across such original ideas nowadays, especially in the genre of science fiction, which makes the disappointment twice as big to see it wasted in clich√©s and cheap tricks. I guess what bothered me most was the fact that Somoza objectified his own heroin. And although the objectification makes sense -quite cleverly, one might say- in the end, the uneasiness remains, highlighted by the lack of a redemptive character development. Another thing that spoiled the fun for me was the way the author served his characters on a plate. Starting with the heroin, he spends four pages describing every bit of detail, from how she looks and what she wears to how she interacts with other people and how they interact with her, forcing us to wave the joy of gradual discovery goodbye. A method he uses with all his characters from start to finish.<br />As for the plot, I‚Äôve grown sick of the trick of interrupting the story at crucial moments just to prolong the suspense. ‚ÄúLook close as I am gonna make a big revelation now‚Ä¶ right after a 20-page chapter dedicated to the bad guy and how he takes his tea.‚Äù I find this method manipulative of the reader‚Äôs emotions and downright irritating.<br />It‚Äôs a shame because, like I said, the idea is brilliant as is the way Somoza handles it. Even the way it ends, although a bit on the easy side, didn‚Äôt disappoint me in the least. I might be wrong, but I feel that if it was written some decades back, its literary value would be as big as its originality.</span>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer182148072" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer17125066880952698966">As others have said, this book's concept of seeing into the past is so interesting that I just long for better treatment of it. For example, from the book:<br/><br/>"Let's suppose we ask the satellites to capture a sequence of the Nile delta. They do it, send it to us, and a computer processes it and obtains a series of maps of the pyramids. After streaming the beam of electrons through our synchrotron, we recover the recently formed particles, and then another computer reconstructs them and captures the </span>
  <span id="freeText17125066880952698966" style="display:none">As others have said, this book's concept of seeing into the past is so interesting that I just long for better treatment of it. For example, from the book:<br /><br />"Let's suppose we ask the satellites to capture a sequence of the Nile delta. They do it, send it to us, and a computer processes it and obtains a series of maps of the pyramids. After streaming the beam of electrons through our synchrotron, we recover the recently formed particles, and then another computer reconstructs them and captures the new image. If we've used the right amount of energy, then we'll be able to see the same place, the Egyptian Pyramids, but, say, three thousand years earlier... with a little luck, for a few seconds, we'll be able to see a pyramid being built, or a pharaoh's burial ceremony."<br /><br />And they do look into the past several times. But then... some scientists mishandle the discovery and unleash an evil entity and the project is shut down. I only wish this book was about the project and not some crazy explanation about how the entity came to be. And why it's killing everyone. Sigh.</span>

<div id="review_183572723" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/183572723" />
    <a title="Alibiserver" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5170302-alibiserver"><img alt="Alibiserver" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1301031580p2/5170302.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/183572723?book_show_action=true">Jul 09, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alibiserver" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alibiserver" href="/user/show/5170302-alibiserver">Alibiserver</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780061193736" href="/book/show/4342761-zig-zag">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer183572723" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12182850390322178299">As I read Somoza‚Äôs Zigzag, I had the feeling that what I‚Äôm currently writing right now (and thinking about it) fell in the similar vein though treading a wholly different concept. Soon, the pages raced on, not until I scratched my head at the ending.<br/><br/>Now, don‚Äôt get me wrong; it doesn‚Äôt mean that when my hands race at the pages in excitement I highly rate the book. Many books had done that to me, either the effect really out of excitement or only out of the sheer desperation to finish it. I had th</span>
  <span id="freeText12182850390322178299" style="display:none">As I read Somoza‚Äôs Zigzag, I had the feeling that what I‚Äôm currently writing right now (and thinking about it) fell in the similar vein though treading a wholly different concept. Soon, the pages raced on, not until I scratched my head at the ending.<br /><br />Now, don‚Äôt get me wrong; it doesn‚Äôt mean that when my hands race at the pages in excitement I highly rate the book. Many books had done that to me, either the effect really out of excitement or only out of the sheer desperation to finish it. I had those mixed feelings as I read Zigzag on. Somoza‚Äôs writing (or translator, I read the English version) is exhilarating and the beginning is merely a front for what seems to be a promisingly speedy reading, and then, like its premise, it began to all get Greek to me.<br /><br />I have prepared a synopsis below for those of you who are too lazy, or find the book too confusing, though please approach it with some caution. I might be wrong too.<br /><br />WARNING: Spoilers Ahead<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br />	The novel starts in 2015 when Sergio Marini, one of the people behind the secret Project Zigzag died in his Milan apartment. Elisa Robledo, one of the original scientists behind the project is shocked at the loss of her former cohort and shocked at her own safety. When she calls Victor Lopera at his house that fateful night, with a butter knife clutched in her hands, she knew she‚Äôd been waiting for that eventful night when she knew she‚Äôs next.<br /><br />	Flash backward to 2005 before it all started. Elisa is a successful graduate student and she is about to take David Blanes‚Äôs summer course in Alighieri University. David Blanes (not the magician of course) is a renowned Physicist and also notorious in that school. Here, she first meets Ricardo Valente Sharpe, another genius, called as Mr. 0.04 because that‚Äôs the difference between her score and his score. Victor, who introduces him to her during conversations, reckons Ric as a slightly erratic, ‚Äúdangerous‚Äù guy, whose objective is to beat everyone else. Many things happen at Elisa before the project started- bearded men make their appearance, a student with a deadline for a journalistic course starts to egg her with questions, Elisa receives increasingly bizarre pornographic spam emails, and Blanes shows off misogynistic tendencies when he refuses Elisa to take part of their conversations.<br /><br />	When she finally knew what the emails were, Elisa goes to the scene conjured by the emails and meets Ric there with the intention to tell her that because of their natural abilities, they were dangerous to the state and are thus monitored. He then takes her to a room where none of the operatives knew, talks her about it, which naturally makes Elisa raise an eyebrow. They then strike a deal about proving the so-called ‚Äúsequoia theory‚Äù and whoever loses will do what the other tells them to do. Elisa agrees.<br /><br />	The symposium was to be the portion where they knew the winner, and of course, Elisa loses the deal (as what she thought it would be) but before she gives in, she tells him that whoever Blanes will be taking to Zurich (where CERN was and his research would supposedly be) will be the winner. At first, Elisa thought she‚Äôll certainly lose because Ric got the part, not until she was phoned and told to pack up and go to Zurich as she and Ric were chosen.<br /><br />	Zurich though was just a front.<br /><br />	From Zurich, they were taken to New Nelson, a few miles away from Maldives. There, funded by the EAGLE Group, they were to partake in a series of experiments dealing with light. The premise here kicks in- time moves forward but the Past and the Present are moving simultaneously, thus there is a possibility that they can view the past as it happens.<br /><br />	Here, Elisa meets the others who take part in the project. They were split in different teams, with Elisa manning the calculations responsible in opening up the side millions of years ago.<br /><br />David Blanes ‚Äì Out to prove his Sequoia Theory, Blanes reveals his soft side to Elisa and is not really the jerk he was.<br />Sergio Marini ‚Äì Blanes‚Äôs partner in the Sequoia Project<br />Reinhard Silberg ‚Äì Another of their partner Physicists and also had a degree as a historian and philosopher.<br />Colin Craig ‚Äì Worked with Ricardo Sharpe before and made the recommendation to put in Ric Valente in the project<br />Ricardo Valente Sharpe ‚Äì The twisted genius, Ric takes part in making calculations to open up Jerusalem during the time of Jesus<br />Nadja Petrovic ‚Äì Elisa‚Äôs albino friend and Clissot‚Äôs cohort, she is a topnotch palaeontologist.<br />Jacqueline Clissot ‚Äì Frenchwoman who is Nadja‚Äôs mentor(?)<br />Rosalyn Reiter ‚Äì (Honestly, I couldn‚Äôt make out what her role was. Ric‚Äôs plaything then?)<br /><br />	When the calculations were finally set in place all wait in anticipation and the awe that follows when the images were rendered takes them all. The project was a success as they speak, but what shocks them was the woman who was in Jerusalem. The images rendered shows a woman whose profile is eaten away and what shows are the disgusting bits. Silberg thinks it was leprosy, as it was a very common disease at that time.<br /><br />	While everyone was convinced that the project was a success, horrible accidents and mishaps soon beleaguered the island. Ric was missing, and Rosalyn was killed in the generator. As was agreed, they had to be evacuated from the island. But more deaths followed by then.<br /><br />	In a scope of a few years, the scientists involved in the project had gone missing. Craig was mysteriously ripped to shreds, Petrovic committed suicide, and Marini too was killed under mysterious circumstances. While EAGLE covered up the project and those involved were placed into therapy, thinking that they were just the psychological after-effects of seeing the past, Elisa begins to think otherwise. At this point, the tail wags the dog.<br /><br />	And when Marini became the latest casualty with Elisa fearing for her life, Victor comes in the process and helps them in any way they can. With stringent control from EAGLE, the ones remaining conduct a meeting on how to ultimately bring down the invisible, culprit down. Carter, the South African guard they had before, slips those who remained, back to New Nelson to settle the score once and for all.<br /><br />	Of course, by the time it ended, it all became a bloody mess. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />	I would‚Äôve prepared to give the novel a 4. The premise was interesting. Somoza churns out a novel that had elements from Dr. Moreau, Final Destination, and Groundhog Day. His writing is frenetic, edgy, atmospheric, dark and mood-enhancing. It is, as what the blurb from the mass market paperback said, not for the fainthearted. That is, until it all gets blown to pieces by the second portion of the novel in 2015.<br /><br />	I‚Äôd take it that I am not a physicist. I once dreamed of it when I was young but my mind was too poor, and my education too bad to even convince me getting the course. I may had been happier if I had all my resources and did become one, but granted that life on me took a different way around and made me embrace the arts more than the sciences convinced me that I would be equally as happy because by then, my realm is different from that.<br /><br />	And I say, without much fanfare, that the book only gave me confusion.<br /><br />	The premise was workable but it didn‚Äôt strike on me. One of the scenes in the book showing Carter‚Äôs confusion in all of this phenomena best exemplifies my frustration. It simply was out of reach. There was another novel that I read this year was All You Need Is Kill by Hiroshi Sakurazaka, which also dealt with time but it worked out for me better than this<br /><br />	Also, the ending sucks.<br /> <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12182850390322178299" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_176782843" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/176782843" />
    <a title="J" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1191925-j"><img alt="J" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/176782843?book_show_action=true">Jun 14, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J" class="user" itemprop="url" name="J" href="/user/show/1191925-j">J</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1191925-j?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer176782843" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10797593740212974735">BEWARE. I DON'T FLAG SPOILERS. BUT I DON'T PUT MY REVIEWS OUT ON ANY FEED, EITHER.<br/><br/>Jacket description is accurate: this is a blend of science, sci-fi, horror and mystery.<br/><br/>String theory of time is center stage - a team of physicists is trying to view the past by unraveling time strings. But their safety all unravels when one ambitious team member tries to unravel a RECENT time string, which is a no-no, as recent time is "unstable."<br/><br/>A menace fr. the past starts stalking the team members. Their projec</span>
  <span id="freeText10797593740212974735" style="display:none">BEWARE. I DON'T FLAG SPOILERS. BUT I DON'T PUT MY REVIEWS OUT ON ANY FEED, EITHER.<br /><br />Jacket description is accurate: this is a blend of science, sci-fi, horror and mystery.<br /><br />String theory of time is center stage - a team of physicists is trying to view the past by unraveling time strings. But their safety all unravels when one ambitious team member tries to unravel a RECENT time string, which is a no-no, as recent time is "unstable."<br /><br />A menace fr. the past starts stalking the team members. Their project is abruptly halted and deep-sixed. (Even tho it has huge applications for the military - what better way to win a war than to visit past time, and so observe the enemy making war plans.)<br /><br />But, the menace is not a creature from Biblical or other ancient time (as author initially misdirects us to think.) In fact, the culprit turns out to be a "slice" or "split" off the mildest, most unprepossessing, most sympathetic physicist of all (Victor - whom security head Carter, a mercenary, calls "Father" as in "Catholic priest.") <br /><br />Reason: while doing unapproved solo research on the group topic, the ambitious scientist targeted a memory from his own teen years (which is recent time, in the context of all time.) In that event, Victor, a friend since childhood, stumbled on Ambitious Guy making out w. Victor's girlfriend.<br /><br />By picking that moment from the past to revisit, ambitious guy accidentally splits off a fragment of Victor -- the jealous monster within the otherwise meek man -- who then hunts down the scientists, almost to the death of all.<br /><br />To end the terror, surviving members of the original research team return to the remote island to reactivate, and permanently, close out the old experiment. Only two are spared: Elisa (heroine physicist who is beautiful but frigid) and macho Carter. The two intensely dislike each other.<br /> <br />On last page, the reader is left to ponder whether, after the pair's victory, they will be marooned for all time, just the 2 of them, on the small island, because of some scientific fluke (a time freeze?) triggered when they destroyed their enemy: who was the animalistic, non-thinking predator "slice" of angry teen-aged Victor.<br /><br />Intriguing book. I didn't really understand all the science; nor which portions of the plot went beyond the actual science. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10797593740212974735" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_309279106" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/309279106" />
    <a title="John Wunder" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2678124-john-wunder"><img alt="John Wunder" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/309279106?book_show_action=true">Apr 09, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John Wunder" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John Wunder" href="/user/show/2678124-john-wunder">John Wunder</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer309279106" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3679633145540955530">A super fast-paced book with an interesting premise, but ruined by a lot of fetishism and an incessant focus on keeping the thrill up by holding out on revealing things just a little too long. Not that holding out on the reader is bad for a thriller, but I think I would have enjoyed it more if I didn't spend 90% of the book wondering what was happening because the only description was that it was horrifying.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer192436752" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17616101563673718278">This is one of the best SciFi books I've read in a very long time. The theory presented in the book is original, and very well thought out(at least to my pseudo-scientific brain). The character are fairly multi-dimensional, and there is actually a sexiness to the book. Which is to say the least rare in SciFi.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_691903105" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/691903105" />
    <a title="Mike" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2201886-mike"><img alt="Mike" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1239392797p2/2201886.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/691903105?book_show_action=true">Aug 11, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mike" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mike" href="/user/show/2201886-mike">Mike</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anyone</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer691903105" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15340857094822994996"><br/>I picked up this book assuming it was some kind of Science Fiction-cum-mystery novel. It‚Äôs not exactly that. It does have a strong SF element, but it has equally strong elements of horror and suspense. Apparently the author does like his death and mayhem. He certainly provides plenty of it here!<br/><br/>Even though <i>Zig Zag</i> was a very different book than I anticipated, I did enjoy reading it. The suspense/mystery kept me guessing right up until the final reveal. The villain wasn‚Äôt the person I felt it was</span>
  <span id="freeText15340857094822994996" style="display:none"><br />I picked up this book assuming it was some kind of Science Fiction-cum-mystery novel. It‚Äôs not exactly that. It does have a strong SF element, but it has equally strong elements of horror and suspense. Apparently the author does like his death and mayhem. He certainly provides plenty of it here!<br /><br />Even though <i>Zig Zag</i> was a very different book than I anticipated, I did enjoy reading it. The suspense/mystery kept me guessing right up until the final reveal. The villain wasn‚Äôt the person I felt it was leading up to (even though he did turn out to be a very sick wacko in the closing chapters), but the author‚Äôs villain was a nice twist.<br /><br />The book uses some ideas about string theory to postulate a method where one can view the past. Not travel into it, but just see images created at prior moments (and saved as ‚Äòstrings‚Äù). The scientists devise a relatively low-power way to excite and ‚Äúunravel‚Äù these strings to that they can be imaged and recorded. Pretty good science extrapolation, there. The book revolves around several scientists who build and run a complex (on a conveniently remote island) to do just that.<br /><br />Of course, nothing in life is so simple. First, the funding comes from a shady (perhaps quasi-illegal) and government-affiliated organization. They also provide top-flight security (and ‚Äúminders‚Äù) for the scientists. It is their island that has been setup for the experiments. Second, the time-viewing has unintended consequences on the viewers and reality (if there were none, it would have been a fairly dry book). Third, the result of the experiments is so powerful that everyone who leaves the island is subject to years of medical tests, drugs and mental manipulation.<br /><br />Since I really don‚Äôt want to spoil the book for those who haven‚Äôt read it, I‚Äôm going to stop talking about the plot and characters here. If you like hard SF books with a positive outcome/worldview then this might not be your cup of future history. (It spans the 90‚Äôs to 2015.) On the other hand, if you don‚Äôt mind some thrill and gore, then you might decide this is very good. I would characterize it as ‚Äúhorror with an SF backbone‚Äù since the balance is more away form SF than I would normally prefer.<br /><br />The translation (from Spanish) seemed very good. Overall, it is a solid book with a good storyline. <b>A bit over three full (3.25) stars.</b> And, now that I know what to expect, I may look up Mr. Somoza‚Äôs other novels.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15340857094822994996" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer638230442" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3414670235734398743">Ooh this was a thriller. It starts in the middle of a crisis, goes back to tell you how the horrors came to be, then drives straight through to an Inception-y conclusion, unraveling secrets on the way. The whole premise is based on a lot of theoretical physics (which may or may not be accurate), and much of it went over my head. I'm good enough with science and willing to allow some temporary suspension of disbelief (good ol' TSOD, as my 8th grade English teacher used to say), so I was good. How</span>
  <span id="freeText3414670235734398743" style="display:none">Ooh this was a thriller. It starts in the middle of a crisis, goes back to tell you how the horrors came to be, then drives straight through to an Inception-y conclusion, unraveling secrets on the way. The whole premise is based on a lot of theoretical physics (which may or may not be accurate), and much of it went over my head. I'm good enough with science and willing to allow some temporary suspension of disbelief (good ol' TSOD, as my 8th grade English teacher used to say), so I was good. However, if you're the type who hates high-level science or loves it and hates when novelists get it wrong, then maybe this isn't the book for you. But if you like scary sci-fi thrillers, then yes. Read it.</span>

<div id="review_2486010394" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2486010394" />
    <a title="Phil" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1369028-phil"><img alt="Phil" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442490466p2/1369028.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2486010394?book_show_action=true">Oct 03, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Phil" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Phil" href="/user/show/1369028-phil">Phil</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2486010394" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3791099277270630033">While the synopsis mentions an interesting idea of using some new application of physics to look into the past, most of the book centered on some weird, fetishist messing with people and systematically tracking them down and killing them. While not a bad premise for a book, I was hoping the book would be more about the "looking into the past" thing, than the serial killer thing. <br/><br/>However, there were other, larger problems with the book:<br/>1. Cliffhangers were forced in at every possible opportunity.</span>
  <span id="freeText3791099277270630033" style="display:none">While the synopsis mentions an interesting idea of using some new application of physics to look into the past, most of the book centered on some weird, fetishist messing with people and systematically tracking them down and killing them. While not a bad premise for a book, I was hoping the book would be more about the "looking into the past" thing, than the serial killer thing. <br /><br />However, there were other, larger problems with the book:<br />1. Cliffhangers were forced in at every possible opportunity. I'm cool with a cliffhanger here and there, but it became such a rampant problem, that I felt like I should just skip to reading the last chapter to see how everything turned out. The cliffhangers weren't organic. They weren't necessary. They were purely included to keep the reader reading. And they were annoying. <br />2. Why are all the women so comfortable being naked, all the time!?!? This isn't some prudish comment. This is a comment about how absolutely unbelievable the concept was that these women would be OK with being watched, naked, over and over and over again. Their living quarters all had windows, so no privacy in the bedroom, bathroom, etc. Men walk in while they are naked for various reasons, and the women's reactions are no more than "oh well, maybe I should cover myself up now...but maybe not". Even post-experiment observations, for experiments that had NOTHING PHYSICAL ABOUT THEM, required watching the women while naked. A woman wants to change into her bathing suit, and rather than changing before getting to the beach, she just drops her clothes right there on the sand and changes there. It was so completely unrealistic, that it made me question whether there was a point to all the nudity (not really) or if the author had the mentality of a 15 year old boy (more likely). <br /><br />If these two issues could have been smoothed out, it would have been an ok read. As it is, I can't recommend it. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3791099277270630033" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2805722262" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2805722262" />
    <a title="Eden Thompson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32724160-eden-thompson"><img alt="Eden Thompson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1404252236p2/32724160.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2805722262?book_show_action=true">May 01, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eden Thompson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eden Thompson" href="/user/show/32724160-eden-thompson">Eden Thompson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2805722262" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13029605786161451724">From my book blog <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://www.JetBlackDragonfly.blogspot.com'>www.JetBlackDragonfly.blogspot.com</a><br/>Zig Zag by Jose Carlos Somoza is ambitious - a thriller, science fiction, horror novel with a secret island laboratory, time travel and supernatural murders. Heavy on the quantum mechanics, it's an intellectual adventure in the style of Michael Crichton. It was certainly original.<br/><br/>Elisa, a sexy Madrid physicist, was once part of a team of researchers lead by a famous Spanish physicist. They were flown to a mysterious island in the Indian Ocean a</span>
  <span id="freeText13029605786161451724" style="display:none">From my book blog <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.JetBlackDragonfly.blogspot.com">www.JetBlackDragonfly.blogspot.com</a><br />Zig Zag by Jose Carlos Somoza is ambitious - a thriller, science fiction, horror novel with a secret island laboratory, time travel and supernatural murders. Heavy on the quantum mechanics, it's an intellectual adventure in the style of Michael Crichton. It was certainly original.<br /><br />Elisa, a sexy Madrid physicist, was once part of a team of researchers lead by a famous Spanish physicist. They were flown to a mysterious island in the Indian Ocean and worked in a lab splitting time strings - not just theorizing, but proving that light streams record pictures throughout time that can be accessed - virtually showing you any split second throughout history. The project has flaws, the lab explodes and everyone is removed, ordered to never speak of the incident. Ten years later, several of the researchers are being killed in bizarre and violent ways and Elisa begins to panic. Her solution: return to the destroyed island lab where it all began. The only explanation too unbelievable: a hyper dimensional villain is disrupting time.<br /><br />This borders the line between an exciting science adventure and, at over 500 pages, becoming boring. I want to like this, but there are massive flaws hard to overlook. Elisa and the female characters are portrayed as sexy to an embarrassing degree, near the end turning into nymphomaniacs, for some reason. Most of the characters are poorly written, perhaps that's the translation from Spanish. He has a habit of jumping from scene to scene, but instead of a cliffhanger I lost the thread and the momentum, leaving me flat. I would like to read a thriller with these elements, but this wasn't it. There are interesting moral consequences, results of disrupting split time streams, that are given the briefest coverage - coupled with an overload of military, science, history, and religion that bogs it down. So, no, it wasn't very good. I wish it was.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13029605786161451724" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer6711413147162535372">The novel <i>Zig Zag</i> written by Jose Carlos Somoza is an interesting depiction of the informative life of Elisa Robledo. Since reading the first chapter, you are left wondering what is really going on. What is going on, one question you will be wondering. Why is she so quiet so secretive?   <span id="freeText6711413147162535372" style="display:none">The novel <i>Zig Zag</i> written by Jose Carlos Somoza is an interesting depiction of the informative life of Elisa Robledo. Since reading the first chapter, you are left wondering what is really going on. What is going on, one question you will be wondering. Why is she so quiet so secretive? You will have to read on to find out.<br /><br />Elisa‚Äôs life was generally normal, she is a theoretical physicist working as a professor at Alighieri University in Madrid, Spain. Her life was good, though she had few friends, but everything changes when she receives an ominous message from an anonymous source saying ‚ÄúYou‚Äôre next‚Äù. She knows exactly what this is about. Our book takes us through the adventures of how she ended up where she was. Strange things are happening. She swears that people are spying on her, she keeps seeing older men was consistent, perfectly groomed mustaches following. Is it something to be alarmed about or not? The reader will have to find out if so.<br /><br />This book is not for the average reader looking for a simple good book. This book for some may be hard to read because of the level that I would hold it at difficulty wise. This book also contains inappropriate content and language that would not be suited for younger readers as well. <i>Zig Zag</i> is an interesting book but I did not exactly enjoy reading it. The character development was almost non-existent and some were uncomfortable to think about. Somewhat the science aspect of this book was there more than the actual plot was. Therefor I would not recommend this book, someone interested in the science aspect might enjoy it, I know that was the most interesting thing for me. This book doesn‚Äôt have a very broad range of the readers age, it was strangely uncomfortable to read which would discourage an older audience, but was too inappropriate for a younger reader. I felt that I fit who this book was aiming towards but yet I still did not enjoy it.<br /></span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/990150644?book_show_action=true">Jul 08, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Agape" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Agape" href="/user/show/7285434-agape">Agape</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer990150644" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9018735246003003281">String theory, physics, seeing into the past.... Sounds really promising, right?! At first, I was really excited to read this book but after a while, I realized that it was going to be another major disappointment. The first half of the book made me feel like I was witnessing some sort of teenage drama, not an accomplished scientist doing her job. I don't want to know about the first time the main character had sex, I don't care about her taste in porn, and again, I couldn't care less about the </span>
  <span id="freeText9018735246003003281" style="display:none">String theory, physics, seeing into the past.... Sounds really promising, right?! At first, I was really excited to read this book but after a while, I realized that it was going to be another major disappointment. The first half of the book made me feel like I was witnessing some sort of teenage drama, not an accomplished scientist doing her job. I don't want to know about the first time the main character had sex, I don't care about her taste in porn, and again, I couldn't care less about the numerous descriptions of her breasts and surely there's no need to remind us every once in a while that she's so hot, yet so intelligent. Who the hell cares?! Just stick to the point and give us some action already!!! 300 tedious pages later and after several desperate attempts to keep the thrill up, things seem to take a turn for the better (people are being killed, there's a huge mystery to solve etc.), but instead of focusing on the murders and the important stuff, the writer decides to skip all the gruesome details and all we get is a tiny description of each murder. Suddenly, the action moves too fast, and from time to time I couldn't even understand what's going on, since many important details are left out and not explained thoroughly. Furthermore, considering the fact that the main theme of the book is string theory, I expected that the writer would at least make an attempt to explain some confusing concepts which may be unfamiliar to some readers. But no, scientific facts weren't important enough to be discussed at length, as only a small part of the book focuses on physics. It would have been a lot better if there were more facts and less pointless chitchat. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9018735246003003281" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer4833533318918361374">7/6/12<br/>I'm intrigued to crack open this book. The cover snagged me on the library shelf and the jacket copy guaranteed I would bring it home. Now, reading over the polar extremes of the reviews on Goodreads, I'm excited to find out how good (or bad) this book really is!<br/><br/>7/15/12<br/>I have to tell you... this was a good book. A solid read (at 525 pages or so) that kept me interested and wondering throughout and provided a satisfying ending. As an author, I started turning my nose up at the author's repe</span>
  <span id="freeText4833533318918361374" style="display:none">7/6/12<br />I'm intrigued to crack open this book. The cover snagged me on the library shelf and the jacket copy guaranteed I would bring it home. Now, reading over the polar extremes of the reviews on Goodreads, I'm excited to find out how good (or bad) this book really is!<br /><br />7/15/12<br />I have to tell you... this was a good book. A solid read (at 525 pages or so) that kept me interested and wondering throughout and provided a satisfying ending. As an author, I started turning my nose up at the author's repeated use of that time-worn cliche': "had she known what would happen next, she would never have..." He used it about 30 times throughout the first 200 pages. But you know what? It did exactly what it's intended to: it cranked up the tension and kept me turning pages. I don't know that I would have the guts to stick something so blatant and laughable into my novel, but it worked, so who am I to complain?<br /><br />Beyond that little idiosyncrasy though, it was a Crichtonesque combination of theoretical physics and good old scare-your-pants-off horror. I tended to cringe at some of the (male) author's obviously shallow treatment of the female characters. Like any B movie would, he made sure they rarely wore anything that wasn't revealing, and he made sure to explain exactly what these ridiculous outfits were revealing as well. Beyond that, they seemed to have little to do or say, even though one of them was the lead.<br /><br />But again, these silly little things aside, I enjoyed the book, I got through it quickly, which is tough for me, and I hope my next read is as much fun!</span>
  <a data-text-id="4833533318918361374" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked

    }
  }

    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }
